Cage molecules are three-dimensional, polycyclic structures that enclose a cavity, allowing them to encapsulate guest species. Common applications for cage molecules include molecular encapsulation, selective ion or molecule transport, drug delivery, gas storage, and as hosts in host-guest chemistry for sensing or catalysis. Macrocycles, on the other hand, are large ring-shaped molecules (such as crown ethers, cyclodextrins, and porphyrins) that can bind ions or molecules within or near their ring structure. Their applications include ion transport, molecular recognition, catalysis, drug delivery, and as building blocks in supramolecular assemblies. There is significant overlap in uses: both cage molecules and macrocycles are employed in host-guest chemistry, molecular recognition, and as selective carriers for ions or small molecules. However, cage molecules are particularly suited for encapsulating larger or more complex guests due to their 3D cavities, while macrocycles are often preferred for selective binding of ions or small molecules within their ring. In summary, while both classes are central to supramolecular chemistry and share applications in molecular recognition and transport, cage molecules offer unique advantages for encapsulation and Cage molecules are three-dimensional, polycyclic structures that enclose a cavity, allowing them to encapsulate guest species. Common applications for cage molecules include molecular encapsulation, selective ion or molecule transport, drug delivery, gas storage, and as hosts in host-guest chemistry for sensing or catalysis. Macrocycles, on the other hand, are large ring-shaped molecules (such as crown ethers, cyclodextrins, and porphyrins) that can bind ions or molecules within or near their ring structure. Their applications include ion transport, molecular recognition, catalysis, drug delivery, and as building blocks in supramolecular assemblies. There is significant overlap in uses: both cage molecules and macrocycles are employed in host-guest chemistry, molecular recognition, and as selective carriers for ions or small molecules. However, cage molecules are particularly suited for encapsulating larger or more complex guests due to their 3D cavities, while macrocycles are often preferred for selective binding of ions or small molecules within their ring. In summary, while both classes are central to supramolecular chemistry and share applications in molecular recognition and transport, cage molecules offer unique advantages for encapsulation and Cage molecules are three-dimensional, polycyclic structures that enclose a cavity, allowing them to encapsulate guest species. Common applications for cage molecules include molecular encapsulation, selective ion or molecule transport, drug delivery, gas storage, and as hosts in host-guest chemistry for sensing or catalysis. Macrocycles, on the other hand, are large ring-shaped molecules (such as crown ethers, cyclodextrins, and porphyrins) that can bind ions or molecules within or near their ring structure. Their applications include ion transport, molecular recognition, catalysis, drug delivery, and as building blocks in supramolecular assemblies. There is significant overlap in uses: both cage molecules and macrocycles are employed in host-guest chemistry, molecular recognition, and as selective carriers for ions or small molecules. However, cage molecules are particularly suited for encapsulating larger or more complex guests due to their 3D cavities, while macrocycles are often preferred for selective binding of ions or small molecules within their ring. In summary, while both classes are central to supramolecular chemistry and share applications in molecular recognition and transport, cage molecules offer unique advantages for encapsulation and Cage molecules are three-dimensional, polycyclic structures that enclose a cavity, allowing them to encapsulate guest species. Common applications for cage molecules include molecular encapsulation, selective ion or molecule transport, drug delivery, gas storage, and as hosts in host-guest chemistry for sensing or catalysis. Macrocycles, on the other hand, are large ring-shaped molecules (such as crown ethers, cyclodextrins, and porphyrins) that can bind ions or molecules within or near their ring structure. Their applications include ion transport, molecular recognition, catalysis, drug delivery, and as building blocks in supramolecular assemblies. There is significant overlap in uses: both cage molecules and macrocycles are employed in host-guest chemistry, molecular recognition, and as selective carriers for ions or small molecules. However, cage molecules are particularly suited for encapsulating larger or more complex guests due to their 3D cavities, while macrocycles are often preferred for selective binding of ions or small molecules within their ring. In summary, while both classes are central to supramolecular chemistry and share applications in molecular recognition and transport, cage molecules offer unique advantages for encapsulation and
